A) Because God is not eternal
B) Because the term implies temporal priority
C) Because God is beyond attributes
D) Because pre-eternity is only for creation

Key Takeaway
The Muammariyya rejected attributing pre-eternity and self-knowledge to God based on linguistic and philosophical arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Muammariyya rejected God's self-knowledge because they thought it violates the distinction between knower and known. In your life, have you ever rejected a truth about God because it seemed contradictory to your reason? What would it take for you to accept that God's knowledge of Himself is beyond your comprehension?
?
Consider the Muammariyya's argument against pre-eternity: they said the term implies time, so it cannot apply to God. When have you used your own limited concepts to judge what can or cannot be said about God? How does this passage call you to submit to whatever names and attributes God has revealed, even if they seem to carry human connotations?
?
The Muammariyya denied two attributes based on rational arguments. Are there any attributes of God that you might be tempted to reinterpret or deny because they don't fit your modern worldview? What will you do about that temptation?

Muammariyya on Human Action: Will Only
States the Muammariyya doctrine on human action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Muammariyya view that human action consists solely of will, both direct and generated.

Core Idea
Man's only act is will.

The Muammariyya adopted a philosophical view of human nature. They held that a person's only real action is the act of will—whether it directly produces an effect or indirectly generates one. This lesson examines that claim and its connection to their overall worldview.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on human action
Read the final sentence of the Arabic passage. Note that it says man has no act other than will, and mentions 'direct or generated'.
The phrase 'directly or generated' refers to whether the will immediately causes an action or produces an effect through some chain.
2
Understand the concept of 'generated' will
The Muammariyya distinguish between direct will (causing immediate action) and generated will (causing an effect indirectly). Both are still will.
This reflects their adoption of philosophical views on causality.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this view reduces human agency to mere intention. The actual physical movements or outcomes are not directly under human control.
This aligns with their denial of God creating bodies and accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?
The Muammariyya teach that your will is the essence of your action. When you stand before Allah, will your will bear witness that you sought His pleasure, or will it reveal a divided heart? Take a moment: what is the current state of your will toward obedience, and what will you do about it starting now?

The Thumamiyya on Generated Acts: No Agent
Thumamiyya's denial of an agent for generated acts and their argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Thumamiyya's argument that generated acts have no agent and why they rejected attributing them to either humans or God.

Core Idea
Generated acts have no agent—neither the dead nor God can be their source.

The Thumamiyya sect, founded by Thumama ibn Ashras, held a controversial view on generated acts (actions that result from human effort but are not directly willed). They argued that such acts cannot be attributed to any agent, using a striking example of a shooter who dies before an arrow strikes its target. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and its implications for Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on generated acts
Read the Arabic and English translation carefully. Focus on the argument about the arrow and the dead shooter.
Notice how the Thumamiyya present two disjuncts: attributing to dead or to God.
2
Examine the two arguments
First, attributing the act to a dead person is impossible because they are no longer an agent. Second, attributing it to God would imply God causes evil, which they reject.
This is a dilemma designed to show no agent exists.
3
Reflect on the theological stakes
Consider why mainstream theologians found this view problematic—it undermines divine causality and human responsibility for generated acts.
This doctrine was a minority position within the Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثمامية: هو ثمامة بن أشرس (النميري) كان جامعاً بين سخافة الدين وخلاعة النفس، قالوا : الأفعال المتولدة لا ( فاعل لها إذ لا يمكن إسنادها إلى فاعل لاستلزامه إسناد الفعل إلى الميت فيما إذا رمى سهما إلى شخص ومات قبل وصوله إليه ولا إلى الله تعالى لاستلزامه صدور القبيح عنه ( والمعرفة متولدة من النظر وإنها واجبة قبل الشرع واليهود والنصارى والمجوس والزنادقة يصيرون في الآخرة ( تراباً لا يدخلون جنة ولا ناراً وكذا البهائم والأطفال، والاستطاعة سلامة الآلة وهي قبل الفعل ( ومن لا يعلم خالقه الكفار معذور والمعارف كلها ضرورية ولا فعل للإنسان غير الإرادة وما عداها حادث بلا محدث والعالم فعل الله بطبعه كأنهم أرادوا به ما يقوله الفلاسفة من الإيجاب ويلزمه قدم العالم، وكان ثمامة في زمان المأمون وله عنده منزلة
The Thumamiyya: He is Thumama ibn Ashras (al-Numayri). He combined frivolity of religion and licentiousness of soul. They said: Generated acts have no agent (فاعل), because it is impossible to attribute them to any agent—attributing the act to the dead when he shoots an arrow at a person and dies before it reaches him, nor to God, because that would entail the occurrence of evil from Him. And knowledge is generated from reflection and is obligatory before revelation. Jews, Christians, Magians, and heretics become in the afterlife dust, entering neither Paradise nor Hell; likewise beasts and children. And ability (الاستطاعة) is soundness of the instrument, and it is before the act. And whoever does not know his Creator—the disbelievers are excused. All cognitions (المعارف) are necessary. And man has no act other than will (الإرادة). Whatever is other than that is a happening without a maker. And the world is the act of God by nature—as if they intended by this what the philosophers say about emanation (الإيجاب), which entails the pre-eternity of the world. And Thumama was in the time of al-Ma'mun and had a high rank with him.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage describes the Thumamiyya sect, its founder Thumama ibn Ashras, and his unflattering characterization. It lists several doctrines: (1) Generated acts (الأفعال المتولدة) have no specific agent: attributing them to a deceased person is problematic, and attributing them to God would entail evil from Him. (2) Knowledge is generated from reflection (النظر) and obligatory before revelation. (3) Jews, Christians, Magians, heretics, beasts, and children become dust in the afterlife—neither Paradise nor Hell. (4) Ability (الاستطاعة) is soundness of the instrument and precedes the act. (5) Disbelievers who do not know their Creator are excused. (6) All cognitions (المعارف) are necessary. (7) Man has only will (الإرادة); everything else happens without a maker. (8) The world is God's act by nature, akin to the philosophers' emanation (الإيجاب), implying the world's pre-eternity. Thumama's historical context (time of al-Ma'mun) is noted.

Key Terms
أفعال متولدة
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Thumamiyya deny all causality for generated acts.
Correction: They deny a specific agent (human or divine) but still accept that generated acts occur; they simply refuse to assign them to an agent.

Checkpoint
What two possibilities does the Thumamiyya consider for the agent of a generated act?
A) The dead person or an angel
B) The dead person or God
C) God or nature
D) The living person or the universe

Key Takeaway
According to the Thumamiyya, generated acts have no agent because attributing them to either the dead or to God leads to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If generated acts have no agent, who is responsible for the consequences of your actions after you die? Consider a promise you made that you cannot fulfill after death. Is that promise binding? How does this lesson shape your understanding of accountability before Allah?
?
The Thumamiyya argue that attributing the arrow's hit to God makes God the author of evil. Reflect on a time you blamed others for unintended consequences of your actions. Are you willing to accept full responsibility, or do you attribute your failures to external factors? How does this lesson challenge your own tendency to shift blame?
?
The dilemma of the dead shooter: actions initiated in life produce effects after death. What legacy are you leaving behind? What 'arrows' have you shot that are still in flight? Are you confident that the final outcome will be to your credit or against you?

Other Thumamiyya Doctrines: Knowledge, Fate, Ability, Will, and the World
Enumerates the Thumamiyya's doctrines on knowledge, afterlife, ability, will, and the world's nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Thumamiyya's views on knowledge, the afterlife, ability, will, and the nature of the world as presented in this passage.

Core Idea
The Thumamiyya held a cluster of heterodox views on knowledge, fate, ability, will, and the world's nature.

Beyond their denial of an agent for generated acts, the Thumamiyya held several other distinctive doctrines. This passage covers their beliefs about knowledge being generated from reflection and obligatory before revelation, the afterlife fate of disbelievers and children, ability as soundness of instruments, the excusing of ignorant disbelievers, the necessity of all cognitions, the reduction of human act to will alone, and the world as God's natural (emanation-like) act. Let's examine these in order.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the doctrines after 'generated acts'. Note each distinct claim.
There are multiple independent doctrines here.
2
Identify the key doctrines
List them: knowledge generation, fate of disbelievers, ability definition, excuse for the ignorant, all cognitions necessary, human act = will, world as God's act by nature.
Some may seem contradictory (e.g., knowledge is obligatory yet disbelievers excused).
3
Reflect on the overall coherence
Consider how these doctrines fit together: a strong naturalism and denial of divine intervention in generated acts, yet an affirmation of the world as God's natural act.

The Khayyatiyya: Power, Nonexistent Things, and Divine Attributes
Lists the beliefs of the Khayyatiyya sect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinctive doctrines of the Khayyatiyya sect regarding power, the ontological status of nonexistent entities, and the reinterpretation of divine attributes as knowledge.

Core Idea
The Khayyatiyya affirmed the reality of nonexistent entities and reinterpreted divine attributes as knowledge.

The Khayyatiyya, followers of Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat, held distinctive views on power, the status of nonexistent things, and divine attributes. Their positions are important for understanding Mu'tazili subsects and debates about the nature of God's attributes and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Notice how each doctrinal point is presented.
2
Identify each key claim
The passage lists six claims. Find each one: power attribution, nonexistent as thing/substance/accident, God's will for His own acts, God's will for servants' acts, hearing/seeing as knowledge, and seeing as knowledge.
3
Reflect on the broader significance
Ask yourself: How does affirming the reality of nonexistent things affect the understanding of creation? And how does viewing attributes as knowledge save divine simplicity?
This connects debates within Mu'tazilism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخياطية: أصحاب أبي الحسين بن أبي عمر والخياطية قالوا : (بالقدر) أي إسناد الأفعال إلى العباد ( وتسمية المعدوم شيئاً ) أي ثابتاً متقرراً في حال العدم ( وجوهراً وعرضاً ) أي الذوات الثابتة متصفة بصفات الأجناس حالة العدم، ( وإن إرادة الله كونه ) قادراً ( غير مكره ولا كاره (وهي أي إرادته تعالى ( في أفعال نفسه الخلق) أي كونه خالقاً لها، ( وفي أفعال عباده الأمر بها وكونه سميعاً بصيراً) معناه أنه عالم بمتعلقهما، وكونه يرى ذاته أو غيره ) معناه ) أنه يعلمه) ،
The Khayyatiyya: The followers of Abu al-Husayn ibn Abi Umar al-Khayyat. They said: (By 'power' (القدر) meaning attributing acts to servants. And (by naming the nonexistent a 'thing') meaning it is established and determined (ثابت متقرر) in the state of non-existence (العدة). And (by naming it a 'substance' and 'accident') meaning the stable essences (الذوات الثابتة) are qualified with genus-attributes in the state of non-existence. And (that God's will concerning His own acts is creation (الخلق)), i.e., He is their creator. And (concerning His servants' acts, it is command (الأمر) by them. And His being Hearing, Seeing means that He is knowledgeable of their objects. And His seeing His self or other means that He knows it.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage details the beliefs of the Khayyatiyya sect. It explains their interpretation of key terms: (1) 'Power' (القدر) means attributing acts to servants. (2) They affirm calling the nonexistent (المعدوم) a 'thing' (شيء), meaning it is established and determined (ثابت متقرر) in non-existence. (3) They call it 'substance' (جوهر) and 'accident' (عرض), meaning that stable essences (الذوات الثابتة) are qualified with genus-attributes even in nonexistence. (4) God's will regarding His own acts is creation (الخلق), i.e., He is the creator of those acts. (5) Regarding His servants' acts, God's will is command (الأمر). (6) God is Hearing and Seeing meaning He knows their objects. (7) God's seeing His self or other means knowledge of it.

Key Terms
قدر
معدوم
شيء
جوهر
عرض
إرادة
سميع بصير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Khayyatiyya deny divine attributes entirely.
Correction: They do not deny attributes but reinterpret them as knowledge, affirming that God is Hearing and Seeing by knowing their objects.

Checkpoint
According to the Khayyatiyya, what does God's will concerning His servants' acts mean?
A) He creates them directly
B) He commands them
C) He forbids them
D) He is indifferent

Key Takeaway
The Khayyatiyya affirm the reality of nonexistent entities and reinterpret divine attributes as knowledge, emphasizing human agency through power attributed to servants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever reduced a divine attribute like 'Seeing' to merely 'knowing' in your mind, just as the Khayyatiyya did? What might you lose in your reverence for God when you strip His attributes of their distinctness? Will you ask Him tonight to help you worship Him as He truly is, not as you imagine Him?
?
The Khayyatiyya called the nonexistent a 'thing' with determination. Do you treat future possibilities—your plans, your worries—as if they are already fixed realities? How does this affect your reliance on God's will over your own? Are you willing to surrender that illusion of control today?
?
You affirm that God's will for your actions is a command, not a compulsion. Do you truly live as though you are responsible for your choices, or do you secretly blame God for your sins? Imagine standing before Him tomorrow: what would your accountability look like if you could no longer shift the blame?

Jahiziyya: All Cognitions Are Necessary and the Nature of Will
Al-Jahiz's views on cognition and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Jahiziyya claim that all cognitions are necessary and their distinctive redefinition of human will as absence of forgetfulness and inclination.

Core Idea
For the Jahiziyya, will is not a separate power but simply awareness or inclination.

The Jahiziyya sect, associated with the eloquent scholar al-Jahiz, held radical epistemological and psychological views. They argued that all human cognitions are necessary—not acquired through choice—and redefined the concept of will in a way that eliminates it as an independent faculty. This passage presents these foundational beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once through. Notice the definition of will for one's own act and for another's.
Pay attention to the parentheses and clarifications.
2
Identify the claim about cognitions
Find the phrase 'المعارف كلها ضرورية' and consider what it means for knowledge to be necessary.
This is a radical epistemological position.
3
Analyze the redefinition of will
Note how will is broken into two types: for one's own act (absence of forgetfulness) and for another's act (inclination). Ask why they deny a separate will.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider how this view affects moral responsibility—if will is just awareness or inclination, what role does choice play?
This connects to broader debates in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجاحظية : هو عمر بن بحر الجاحظ ) من الفضلاء البلغاء في أيام المعتصم والمتوكل وقد طالع كتب الفلاسفة كثيراً من مقالاتهم بعباراته البليغة اللطيفة (قالوا : المعارف كلها ضرورية ولا إرادة في الشاهد ( أي في الواحد منا ( إنما هي ( إرادته لفعله ( عدم السهو) أي كونه عالماً به غير ساه عنه (و) إرادته ( لفعل الغير ) هي (الميل) أي ميل النفس إليه، و) قالوا : (إن) في الأجسام ذوات طبائع مختلفة لها آثار مخصوصة كما ذهب إليه الفلاسفة الطبيعيون ( ويمتنع انعدام الجوهر) إنما تتبدل الأعراض والجواهر باقية على حالها كما قيل في الهيولى ) والنار تجتذب إليها أهلها لا أن الله يدخلها ) أي يدخلهم فيها ، ( والخير والشر من فعل العبد، والقرآن جسد ينقلب تارة رجلا وتارة امرأة .
The Jahiziyya: He is Amr ibn Bahr al-Jahiz, one of the virtuous eloquent speakers in the time of al-Mu'tasim and al-Mutawakkil. He studied the books of the philosophers and took many of their sayings with his eloquent subtle expressions. They said: All cognitions are necessary. And there is no will (إرادة) in the witnessed (الشاهد), i.e., in any one of us; rather, his will for his act is the absence of forgetfulness (عدم السهو), i.e., being knowledgeable of it, not forgetful of it. And his will for another's act is inclination (الميل), i.e., the soul's inclination towards it. And they said: In bodies, there are different natures with specific effects, as the natural philosophers held. And the annihilation of substance (الجوهر) is impossible; only accidents change, while substances remain as they are, as was said about hyle (الهيولى). And fire attracts its people to it, not that God puts them in it. And good and evil are from the act of the servant. And the Quran is a body that transforms sometimes into a man and sometimes into a woman.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage presents the doctrines of the Jahiziyya sect associated with Amr ibn Bahr al-Jahiz, a famed eloquent writer. Key beliefs: (1) All cognitions (المعارف) are necessary. (2) In humans (الشاهد), will (الإرادة) is not a separate faculty; rather, one's will for their own act is absence of forgetfulness (عدم السهو), meaning being aware and not forgetful. One's will for another's act is inclination (الميل) of the soul. (3) Bodies have different natures (طبائع) with specific effects, akin to natural philosophers. (4) Substance (الجوهر) cannot be annihilated; only accidents change while substances persist, like hyle (prime matter). (5) Fire attracts its inhabitants, not that God puts them in it. (6) Good and evil come from the servant's act. (7) The Quran is a body that changes form, sometimes a man, sometimes a woman.

Key Terms
معارف ضرورية
إرادة
عدم السهو
ميل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Jahiziyya completely deny any form of will.
Correction: They do not deny will outright but reinterpret it as awareness or inclination.

Checkpoint
According to the Jahiziyya, what is a person's will for his own act?
A) A separate power created by God
B) The absence of forgetfulness
C) An inclination of the soul
D) An illusion

Key Takeaway
The Jahiziyya taught that all cognitions are necessary and that human will is merely awareness (for one's own action) or inclination (for another's action).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you did something while your mind was elsewhere—not truly aware? According to the Jahiziyya, that act was not fully willed. How does that sit with your sense of accountability for it?
?
If will is merely awareness or inclination, what does that say about the habits you do without thinking? Are you willing to own those actions as truly yours?
?
The Jahiziyya claim all cognitions are necessary. Think of a time you 'learned' something passively, without effort. Did that knowledge change you? If not, what does that reveal about the role of your will in receiving truth?

Jahiziyya: Materialistic Views on Nature and the Quran
Jahiziyya doctrines on natural properties, substance, fire, and Quran as body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Jahiziyya doctrines on natural properties in bodies, the indestructibility of substance, the mechanism of fire, and the corporeal nature of the Quran.

Core Idea
The Jahiziyya adopted naturalistic and materialistic views, even treating the Quran as a physical body.

The Jahiziyya extended their rationalism to the physical world, arguing that bodies have inherent natures with specific effects, that substance cannot be annihilated, and that fire naturally attracts people rather than God casting them in. Most strikingly, they described the Quran as a physical body that can change form. This passage presents these materialistic views.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage, noting the different claims: natural properties, substance, fire, good/evil, Quran as body.
The passage continues from the previous lesson.
2
Analyze the borrowing from natural philosophers
Identify the phrase 'كما ذهب إليه الفلاسفة الطبيعيون' and consider the influence of Greek thought.
3
Examine the substance doctrine
Note the claim that substance cannot be annihilated; only accidents change. Compare to the concept of hyle (الهيولى).
This aligns with Aristotelian matter theory.
4
Consider the Quran as body
Find the statement about the Quran being a body that changes form. Reflect on why this is controversial in Islamic theology.
This contradicts the orthodox view of the Quran as uncreated speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجاحظية : هو عمر بن بحر الجاحظ ) من الفضلاء البلغاء في أيام المعتصم والمتوكل وقد طالع كتب الفلاسفة كثيراً من مقالاتهم بعباراته البليغة اللطيفة (قالوا : المعارف كلها ضرورية ولا إرادة في الشاهد ( أي في الواحد منا ( إنما هي ( إرادته لفعله ( عدم السهو) أي كونه عالماً به غير ساه عنه (و) إرادته ( لفعل الغير ) هي (الميل) أي ميل النفس إليه، و) قالوا : (إن) في الأجسام ذوات طبائع مختلفة لها آثار مخصوصة كما ذهب إليه الفلاسفة الطبيعيون ( ويمتنع انعدام الجوهر) إنما تتبدل الأعراض والجواهر باقية على حالها كما قيل في الهيولى ) والنار تجتذب إليها أهلها لا أن الله يدخلها ) أي يدخلهم فيها ، ( والخير والشر من فعل العبد، والقرآن جسد ينقلب تارة رجلا وتارة امرأة .
The Jahiziyya: He is Amr ibn Bahr al-Jahiz, one of the virtuous eloquent speakers in the time of al-Mu'tasim and al-Mutawakkil. He studied the books of the philosophers and took many of their sayings with his eloquent subtle expressions. They said: All cognitions are necessary. And there is no will (إرادة) in the witnessed (الشاهد), i.e., in any one of us; rather, his will for his act is the absence of forgetfulness (عدم السهو), i.e., being knowledgeable of it, not forgetful of it. And his will for another's act is inclination (الميل), i.e., the soul's inclination towards it. And they said: In bodies, there are different natures with specific effects, as the natural philosophers held. And the annihilation of substance (الجوهر) is impossible; only accidents change, while substances remain as they are, as was said about hyle (الهيولى). And fire attracts its people to it, not that God puts them in it. And good and evil are from the act of the servant. And the Quran is a body that transforms sometimes into a man and sometimes into a woman.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage presents the doctrines of the Jahiziyya sect associated with Amr ibn Bahr al-Jahiz, a famed eloquent writer. Key beliefs: (1) All cognitions (المعارف) are necessary. (2) In humans (الشاهد), will (الإرادة) is not a separate faculty; rather, one's will for their own act is absence of forgetfulness (عدم السهو), meaning being aware and not forgetful. One's will for another's act is inclination (الميل) of the soul. (3) Bodies have different natures (طبائع) with specific effects, akin to natural philosophers. (4) Substance (الجوهر) cannot be annihilated; only accidents change while substances persist, like hyle (prime matter). (5) Fire attracts its inhabitants, not that God puts them in it. (6) Good and evil come from the servant's act. (7) The Quran is a body that changes form, sometimes a man, sometimes a woman.

Key Terms
طبائع
جوهر
هيولى
قرآن جسد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Jahiziyya denied the Quran's divine origin.
Correction: They did not deny it is from God; they considered it a physical body.

Checkpoint
According to the Jahiziyya, what happens to substances (جواهر) when accidents change?
A) They are annihilated and recreated
B) They remain as they are
C) They transform into other substances
D) They become non-existent

Key Takeaway
The Jahiziyya held a materialistic worldview: bodies have natural properties, substance is indestructible, fire acts naturally, and the Quran is a physical body that changes form.

The Ka'biyya: Rethinking Divine Will and Vision
Lists the Ka'biyya doctrines on divine will and vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ka'biyya sect's distinctive doctrines regarding God's will and vision, and how they reinterpret the attributes of will and sight.

Core Idea
The Ka'biyya reduced God's will to either creation or command, and denied vision of anything.

The Ka'biyya, a Mu'tazili subsect, held radical views on divine attributes. They denied that God has an eternal will as an attribute, instead interpreting 'will' in relation to His acts as creation, and in relation to others' acts as command. Furthermore, they denied that God can see anything, including Himself. This passage outlines these key positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a brief biography followed by four doctrinal claims.
2
Identify the four doctrines
List the four claims: (1) God's act occurs without will, (2) 'willing' for His acts means creator, (3) 'willing' for others' acts means commander, (4) God does not see Himself or others, only knows.
3
Understand the reinterpretation of 'will'
The Ka'biyya do not deny the term 'willing' but give it two distinct meanings depending on the object: creation for His own acts, command for others' acts.
This avoids affirming a separate attribute of will.
4
Note the denial of vision
The Ka'biyya deny that God sees anything, consistent with their emphasis on knowledge alone. They align with the Khayyatiyya on this point.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this view differs from mainstream Mu'tazilism and Sunnism. Why would a sect limit God's attributes so strictly?
This helps contextualize later theological debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكعبية : هو أبو القاسم بن محمد الكعبي كان من معتزلة بغداد وتلميذ ) الخياط ( قالوا : فعل الرب واقع بغير إرادته ( فإذا قيل : إِنه تعالى مريد لأفعاله أريد أنه خالق لها، وإذا قيل : مريد لأفعال غيره أريد أنه آمر بها ) ولا يرى نفسه ولا غيره إلا أنه يعلمه ) كما ذهب إليه الخياطية )
The Ka'biyya: He is Abu al-Qasim ibn Muhammad al-Ka'bi. He was one of the Mu'tazila of Baghdad and a student of al-Khayyat. They said: The act of the Lord occurs without His will (إرادة). When it is said that He is willing (مريد) regarding His acts, it means that He is their creator; and when it is said that He is willing regarding the acts of others, it means that He is the commander (آمر) of them. And He does not see Himself nor others, except that He knows them—as the Khayyatiyya held.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage covers the Ka'biyya sect, named after Abu al-Qasim al-Ka'bi, a student of al-Khayyat. Their key doctrines: (1) God's act occurs without His will (إرادة). (2) When we say God is willing (مريد) regarding His own acts, it means He is their creator (خالق). (3) When we say God is willing regarding others' acts, it means He is their commander (آمر). (4) God does not see Himself nor others, but He knows them. This last point matches the Khayyatiyya view.

Key Terms
إرادة
مريد
خالق
آمر
رؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ka'biyya completely deny God's will, refusing to use the term 'willing' for God.
Correction: The Ka'biyya do use the term 'willing' (مريد) but redefine it as either creator or commander, depending on whether the object is God's act or another's act. They do not deny the term but reinterpret its meaning.

Checkpoint
According to the Ka'biyya, what does it mean to say God is willing (مريد) regarding His own acts?
A) He has a will distinct from His essence
B) He is their creator
C) He commands them
D) He approves them

Key Takeaway
The Ka'biyya sect reinterpreted God's will as either creation or command, and denied that God sees anything, limiting divine attributes to knowledge and action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Ka'biyya reinterpreted God's will to avoid affirming an eternal attribute. In your own life, do you ever rationalize away divine attributes that are difficult to reconcile with your experiences? Do you truly believe God's will is over all things, even the suffering you see? Today, ask God to increase your conviction in His perfect attributes, including His will and vision, even when your understanding fails.
?
The Ka'biyya denied that God sees Himself or others, limiting His knowledge. But Allah calls Himself al-Basir. Do you live as if God sees you in every moment, or is your awareness of His sight dim? Write one specific action you will change today to act in full consciousness of His ever-present vision.

Jubba'iyya on Divine Will and Speech
Jubba'iyya on God's will, annihilation of world, and created speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Jubba'iyya doctrine that God's will and speech are created accidents not inhering in God.

Core Idea
For the Jubba'iyya, God's will and speech are created, not eternal.

The Jubba'iyya were a Mu'tazili sect founded by Abu Ali al-Jubba'i. They held distinctive views on divine attributes, particularly that God's will is a created accident not located in Him. This passage lays out their beliefs on God's will, the annihilation of the world, and the nature of divine speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on divine will
Read the first part of the passage carefully, noting how the Jubba'iyya define God's will as a happening (حادثة) not in a locus.
This rejects the idea that God's will is eternal.
2
Understand the implications for the world's annihilation
Notice that they extend the same logic to the annihilation of the world: it perishes with a perishing not in a locus when God wills it.
This maintains consistency with their concept of will.
3
Analyze the doctrine of divine speech
The passage states that God speaks with speech composed of letters and sounds, created in a body. The speaker is the one who creates the speech, not the one in whom it subsists.
This means God is not the locus of speech; rather, He creates it in some physical entity.
4
Reflect on the denial of seeing God
The Jubba'iyya deny that God will be seen in the afterlife, consistent with their view that God is not physical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجبائية : هو أبو علي ) محمد بن عبد الوهاب ( الجبائي ) من معتزلة البصرة ( قالوا : إرادة الرب حادثة لا في محل والله تعالى مريد بتلك الإرادة موصوف بها والعالم يفنى بفناء لا في محل) عند إرادة الله تعالى فناء العالم، والله متكلم بكلام مركب من حروف وأصوات ) يخلقه ) الله تعالى ( في جسم، والمتكلم بذلك الكلام من فعل الكلام وخلقه لا من قام به وحل فيه، ولا يرى ) الله (في الآخرة والعبد خالق لفعله ومرتكب الكبيرة لا مؤمن ولا كافر، وإذا مات بلا توبة يخلد في النار ولا كرامات للأولياء، ويجب على الله لمن يكلف) أي للمكلف إكمال عقله وتهيئة أسباب التكاليف (له أي يجب عليه اللطف بالمكلف ورعاية ما هو أصلح له ) والأنبياء معصومون : وشارك ) أبو علي ( فيها ) أي في الأحكام المذكورة ( أبا هاشم ثم انفرد) عنه ( بأن الله عالم ) لذاته (بلا ) إيجاب (صفة) هي علم ) ولا حالة توجب العالمية وكونه تعالى ( سمعياً بصيراً) معناه ( أنه لا آفة به، ويجوز الإيلام للعوض
The Jubba'iyya: He is Abu Ali Muhammad ibn Abd al-Wahhab al-Jubba'i, one of the Mu'tazila of Basra. They said: The will of the Lord is a happening (حادثة) not in a locus (لا في محل), and God—exalted—is willing with that will and described by it. And the world perishes with a perishing not in a locus when God wills the annihilation of the world. And God speaks with a speech composed of letters and sounds; He creates it in a body, and the speaker of that speech is the one who performs and creates the speech, not the one in whom it subsists or inheres. And God will not be seen in the afterlife. And the servant is the creator of his own act. And the committer of a major sin is neither a believer nor a disbeliever. And if he dies without repentance, he will abide eternally in Hell. And there are no miracles (كرامات) for saints. And it is obligatory upon God for the one He charges—i.e., the charged person—to perfect his intellect and prepare the means of obligations. And it is obligatory upon Him to grant grace (لطف) to the charged person and to care for what is most beneficial for him. And the prophets are infallible. And Abu Ali shared with Abu Hashim in these mentioned judgments; then he differed from him in that God is knowing by His essence without the affirmation of an attribute that is knowledge, and without a state that necessitates knowingness. And His being Hearing, Seeing means that there is no defect in Him. And it is permissible to inflict pain in return for compensation (العوض).
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage details the doctrines of the Jubba'iyya, the followers of Abu Ali al-Jubba'i, a Basran Mu'tazili. Key points: (1) God's will is a created accident (حادثة) not in a locus (لا في محل); God is described as willing by that accident. (2) The world perishes with a perishing not in a locus when God wills its annihilation. (3) God speaks with speech composed of letters and sounds; He creates it in a body, and the speaker is the one who creates the speech, not the one in whom it subsists. (4) God will not be seen in the afterlife. (5) The servant creates his own act. (6) The committer of a major sin is in an intermediate state (not believer, not disbeliever) and if unrepentant, eternal in Hell. (7) No miracles (كرامات) for saints. (8) God must perfect the intellect of the morally responsible and provide grace (لطف) and what is most beneficial. (9) Prophets are infallible. (10) Abu Ali shared these views with his son Abu Hashim, but differed on: God is knowing by essence without attributing knowledge as a separate attribute or state; His being Hearing and Seeing means no defect; inflicting pain is permissible if compensation is given.

Key Terms
إرادة حادثة
لا في محل
كلام مركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Jubba'iyya deny that God speaks at all.
Correction: They assert that God speaks by creating speech in a body, not by having speech as an attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the Jubba'iyya, what is the nature of God's will?
A) Eternal and uncreated
B) A happening (حادثة) not in a locus
C) Identical to His essence
D) An attribute of action

Key Takeaway
The Jubba'iyya believe God's will and speech are created accidents, not eternal attributes inhering in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will is a created accident not inhering in Him, does that mean He doesn't truly will your existence? How would you reconcile that with your personal sense of purpose under God's direction?
?
The Jubba'iyya say God creates speech in a body, but He is not the speaker in the sense of speech inhering in Him. If the Qur'an is God's speech only in the sense of being a created object, can you still feel that God is directly addressing you when you recite it? What would that do to your conviction in its authority?
?
They deny the beatific vision. If you believed you would never see God in the afterlife, would your longing for Him change? How does your hope of seeing Him shape your worship right now?

Jubba'iyya on Human Actions and Sin
Jubba'iyya on human agency, major sin, and denial of saintly miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Jubba'iyya views on human agency, major sins, and the denial of saintly miracles.

Core Idea
Humans create their own acts, and major sinners face eternal punishment unless they repent.

The Jubba'iyya held strong views on human free will and responsibility. They believed that humans create their own actions, and they defined the status of those who commit major sins as an intermediate category. This passage also denies supernatural miracles for saints.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on human acts
Read the second part of the passage focusing on the servant's creation of his act and the status of the major sinner.
2
Examine the doctrine of human agency
The Jubba'iyya assert that the servant creates his own act, which is a key Mu'tazili point. Consider how this differs from predestinarian views.
3
Understand the fate of the major sinner
Note the intermediate state: not believer, not disbeliever. Also note the eternal punishment if unrepentant.
4
Note the denial of saintly miracles
They deny miracles for saints, reserving them for prophets.

Source Text

Jubba'iyya on Divine Obligations and Unique Doctrines
Jubba'iyya on divine obligations, grace, and unique doctrines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Jubba'iyya views on divine obligations, grace, and their distinctive doctrines on God's attributes and pain.

Core Idea
God must perfect intellects, grant grace, and can inflict pain with compensation.

The Jubba'iyya held that certain obligations are incumbent upon God, reflecting their rationalist approach. They also differed from Abu Hashim on divine attributes. This passage details these ideas, including the infallibility of prophets and the permissibility of inflicting pain if compensated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on divine obligations
Read the last part of the passage on what God must do: perfect intellect, provide grace, and what is most beneficial.
2
Understand the concept of grace (لطف)
The Jubba'iyya believe it is obligatory upon God to grant grace to the charged person. This is a rational obligation.
3
Identify the differences with Abu Hashim
Note that Abu Ali shared most views with his son but disagreed on whether God's knowledge is by essence without attribute, and on the meaning of hearing and sight.
4
Reflect on the permission to inflict pain
The passage ends with the permissibility of inflicting pain if compensated, showing their view on divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Jubba'iyya: He is Abu Ali Muhammad ibn Abd al-Wahhab al-Jubba'i, one of the Mu'tazila of Basra. They said: The will of the Lord is a happening (حادثة) not in a locus (لا في محل), and God—exalted—is willing with that will and described by it. And the world perishes with a perishing not in a locus when God wills the annihilation of the world. And God speaks with a speech composed of letters and sounds; He creates it in a body, and the speaker of that speech is the one who performs and creates the speech, not the one in whom it subsists or inheres. And God will not be seen in the afterlife. And the servant is the creator of his own act. And the committer of a major sin is neither a believer nor a disbeliever. And if he dies without repentance, he will abide eternally in Hell. And there are no miracles (كرامات) for saints. And it is obligatory upon God for the one He charges—i.e., the charged person—to perfect his intellect and prepare the means of obligations. And it is obligatory upon Him to grant grace (لطف) to the charged person and to care for what is most beneficial for him. And the prophets are infallible. And Abu Ali shared with Abu Hashim in these mentioned judgments; then he differed from him in that God is knowing by His essence without the affirmation of an attribute that is knowledge, and without a state that necessitates knowingness. And His being Hearing, Seeing means that there is no defect in Him. And it is permissible to inflict pain in return for compensation (العوض).
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage details the doctrines of the Jubba'iyya, the followers of Abu Ali al-Jubba'i, a Basran Mu'tazili. Key points: (1) God's will is a created accident (حادثة) not in a locus (لا في محل); God is described as willing by that accident. (2) The world perishes with a perishing not in a locus when God wills its annihilation. (3) God speaks with speech composed of letters and sounds; He creates it in a body, and the speaker is the one who creates the speech, not the one in whom it subsists. (4) God will not be seen in the afterlife. (5) The servant creates his own act. (6) The committer of a major sin is in an intermediate state (not believer, not disbeliever) and if unrepentant, eternal in Hell. (7) No miracles (كرامات) for saints. (8) God must perfect the intellect of the morally responsible and provide grace (لطف) and what is most beneficial. (9) Prophets are infallible. (10) Abu Ali shared these views with his son Abu Hashim, but differed on: God is knowing by essence without attributing knowledge as a separate attribute or state; His being Hearing and Seeing means no defect; inflicting pain is permissible if compensation is given.

Key Terms
لطف
أصلح
عوض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Jubba'iyya believe God is bound by obligations in the same way humans are.
Correction: They assert that God obligates Himself rationally; it is a necessary aspect of His justice.

Checkpoint
According to the Jubba'iyya, what does it mean that God is Hearing and Seeing?
A) He has ears and eyes
B) He has no defect
C) He hears and sees literally
D) He creates hearing and seeing

Key Takeaway
The Jubba'iyya held that God must perfect intellects and provide grace, and that His attributes like hearing and sight signify no defect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God does what is most beneficial for you, even when you face pain and hardship? How would your life change if you held this conviction unshakably? This week, when a trial comes, pause and affirm that God's choice is أصلح (best) for you.
?
The Jubba'iyya say God must perfect your intellect. Are you using the intellect God gave you to seek knowledge of Him? What one step can you take today to engage your intellect in learning your creed?
?
If God may inflict pain with compensation, does that change how you view your past sufferings? Will you entrust the compensation to God and release your resentment? Write down one hurt you will surrender to Him now.

Blame Without Disobedience: A Bahshamiyya Doctrine
States the Bahshamiyya view on blame without disobedience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Bahshamiyya position that a person can deserve blame and punishment without committing disobedience, and why this is considered controversial.

Core Idea
Blame can be deserved even without disobedience? The Bahshamiyya say yes.

Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i broke from his father on a key point: he argued that blame and punishment can be merited without any act of disobedience. This doctrine, though noted as contrary to consensus and wisdom, is a hallmark of the Bahshamiyya school. Let's examine the claim and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the claim: blame without disobedience.
2
Identify the claim
The passage states that Abu Hashim differed from his father by asserting that blame and punishment can be deserved without disobedience.
3
Note the opposition
The author adds that this view is contrary to consensus and wisdom. Consider why it might be problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البهمشية : انفرد أبو هاشم عن أبيه بإمكان استحقاق الذم والعقاب بلا معصية) مع كونه مخالفاً للإجماع والحكمة، ( وبأنه لا توبة عن كبيرة مع الإصرار على غيرها عالما بقبحه ويلزمه أن لا يصح إعلام الكافر مع أدنى ذنب أصر عليه (ولا) توبة ( مع عدم القدرة ) فلا تصح توبة الكاذب عن كذبه بعدما صار أخرس، ولا توبة الزاني عن زناه بعدما جب ( ولا يتعلق علم) واحد بمعلومين على التفصيل ولله أحوال لا معلومة ولا مجهولة ولا قديمة ولا حادثة ) قال الآمدي : هذا تناقض إذ لا معنى لكون الشيء حادثاً إلا أنه ليس قديماً ولا معنى لكونه مجهولاً إلا أنه ليس معلوماً على أن إثبات حالة له غير معلومة مما لا سبيل إليه .
The Bahshamiyya: Abu Hashim differed from his father by asserting the possibility of deserving blame and punishment without disobedience—despite it being contrary to consensus and wisdom. And by asserting that there is no repentance from a major sin while persisting in another major sin, knowing its ugliness. And it follows from this that informing the unbeliever (إعلام الكافر) would not be valid with the least sin he persists on. And there is no repentance when unable [to perform the opposite], so the repentance of a liar from his lie after he becomes mute is invalid, and the repentance of an adulterer from his adultery after he is castrated is invalid. And a single knowledge does not relate to two known objects in detail. And God has states (أحوال) that are neither known nor unknown, neither eternal nor originated. Al-Amidi said: This is a contradiction, for there is no meaning to a thing being originated except that it is not eternal, and no meaning to it being unknown except that it is not known. Moreover, affirming a state for Him that is unknown is impossible.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage presents the views of the Bahshamiyya, the school of Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i, who differed from his father (Abu Ali) on several points. The doctrines: (1) A person can deserve blame and punishment without committing disobedience—this is noted as against consensus and wisdom. (2) Repentance from a major sin is not accepted if one persists in another major sin while knowing its ugliness. The consequence is that informing an unbeliever (to guide him) would not be valid if he persists in any sin. (3) Repentance is invalid if the person cannot perform the opposite action (e.g., a liar who becomes mute cannot repent by truthfulness; an adulterer castrated cannot repent by abstaining). (4) A single act of knowledge cannot relate to two distinct objects in detail. (5) God has states (أحوال) that are neither known nor unknown, neither eternal nor originated. The author then quotes al-Amidi who criticizes this as contradictory, because 'originated' means 'not eternal' and 'unknown' means 'not known'. Furthermore, positing an unknown state for God is impossible.

Key Terms
ذم
عقاب
معصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that blame only follows sin.
Correction: The Bahshamiyya argued blame can occur without disobedience.

Checkpoint
According to the Bahshamiyya, what can a person deserve without disobedience?
A) Reward
B) Blame and punishment
C) Forgiveness
D) Divine pleasure

Key Takeaway
The Bahshamiyya held that blame and punishment can be deserved even without sin, a view they claimed was opposed to consensus and wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt blamed or guilty for something that wasn't a direct act of disobedience? How does the Bahshamiyya doctrine challenge your understanding of justice—both divine and human?
?
If blame is possible without sin, how would that change the way you judge yourself and others? Are you willing to examine your own sense of guilt in light of this teaching?
?
This lesson says the Bahshamiyya view is contrary to consensus and wisdom. What does that mean for your trust in the collective understanding of scholars? When have you held a belief that seemed wise but was actually out of step with truth?

Repentance and Ability: Bahshamiyya Conditions
Lists conditions for repentance according to Bahshamiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Bahshamiyya conditions for repentance: that one cannot repent from one major sin while persisting in another, and that repentance requires ability to perform the opposite action.

Core Idea
Sincere repentance is not enough; one must also have the ability to change.

The Bahshamiyya placed strict conditions on repentance. They argued that repentance from a major sin is invalid if the person persists in another major sin while knowing its ugliness. Moreover, repentance requires the ability to perform the opposite act, so a liar who goes mute cannot repent, nor can a castrated adulterer. Let's explore these rulings and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the conditions for repentance as listed by the Bahshamiyya.
2
Identify the two conditions
The first condition: no repentance from one major sin while persisting in another. The second: repentance requires ability to perform the opposite act.
3
Examine the examples
The passage gives examples of a mute liar and a castrated adulterer. Understand why their repentance is invalid.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider the consequence for guiding an unbeliever: if he persists in any sin, informing him is invalid.

Source Text

God's States and Knowledge: Bahshamiyya and al-Amidi's Critique
States Bahshamiyya on knowledge and divine states, with al-Amidi's criticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Bahshamiyya views on divine knowledge and states (ahwal) and al-Amidi's objection that affirming unknowable states is contradictory.

Core Idea
God has states that are beyond human classification, but is this a contradiction?

The Bahshamiyya held two related doctrines: that a single act of knowledge cannot pertain to two distinct objects in detail, and that God has states (ahwal) that are neither known nor unknown, neither eternal nor originated. Al-Amidi criticized this as contradictory, because the definitions of these terms preclude their negation. Let's examine this theological puzzle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the two Bahshamiyya doctrines and al-Amidi's critique.
2
Extract the first doctrine
One act of knowledge cannot relate to two objects in detail.
3
Extract the second doctrine
God has states that are neither known nor unknown, nor eternal nor originated.
4
Understand al-Amidi's critique
He argues that the negations are contradictory because terms are defined by their opposites. Also, affirming an unknown state is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البهمشية : انفرد أبو هاشم عن أبيه بإمكان استحقاق الذم والعقاب بلا معصية) مع كونه مخالفاً للإجماع والحكمة، ( وبأنه لا توبة عن كبيرة مع الإصرار على غيرها عالما بقبحه ويلزمه أن لا يصح إعلام الكافر مع أدنى ذنب أصر عليه (ولا) توبة ( مع عدم القدرة ) فلا تصح توبة الكاذب عن كذبه بعدما صار أخرس، ولا توبة الزاني عن زناه بعدما جب ( ولا يتعلق علم) واحد بمعلومين على التفصيل ولله أحوال لا معلومة ولا مجهولة ولا قديمة ولا حادثة ) قال الآمدي : هذا تناقض إذ لا معنى لكون الشيء حادثاً إلا أنه ليس قديماً ولا معنى لكونه مجهولاً إلا أنه ليس معلوماً على أن إثبات حالة له غير معلومة مما لا سبيل إليه .

And by asserting that there is no repentance from a major sin while persisting in another major sin, knowing its ugliness. And it follows from this that informing the unbeliever (إعلام الكافر) would not be valid with the least sin he persists on. And there is no repentance when unable [to perform the opposite], so the repentance of a liar from his lie after he becomes mute is invalid, and the repentance of an adulterer from his adultery after he is castrated is invalid. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Shi'a: Imamate by Explicit or Implicit Text
Defines the Shi'a as those who believe Ali is the Imam by divine designation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shi'a belief that the Imamate is based on divine designation (النص) and can be explicit or implicit.

Core Idea
The Shi'a hold that Ali was designated Imam by the Prophet, either openly or secretly.

The second major sect among Islamic groups is the Shi'a. Their defining belief is that the Imamate is not a matter of community election but of divine appointment through the Prophet's designation. This passage outlines that belief, explaining the nature of the designation and its implications for the lineage of Imams.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms that define the Shi'a position.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the terms شيعة, إمامة, نص, جلي, خفي, تقية. Reflect on how each term shapes the Shi'a doctrine.
These terms are central to understanding the Shi'a view of leadership.
3
Reflect on exclusivity
Consider why the Shi'a believe Imamate cannot leave Ali's descendants. Note the two exceptions: oppression or dissimulation.
This belief distinguishes Shi'a from Sunni views on caliphate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة الثانية : ( من كبار الفرق الإسلامية ( الشيعة : ( أي الذين شايعوا عليا وقالوا : إنه الإمام بعد رسول الله بالنص إما جلياً وإما خفياً، واعتقدوا أن الإمامة لا تخرج عنه وعن أولاده وإن خرجت فإما بظلم يكون من غيرهم وإما بتقية منه أو من أولاده
The second group among the major Islamic sects: the Shi'a—i.e., those who followed Ali and said that he is the Imam after the Messenger of God by designation (النص), either explicit (جلي) or hidden (خفي), and they believed that the Imamate does not depart from him and his children; and if it departs, it is either due to oppression from others or due to dissimulation (تقية) from him or his children.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage introduces the Shi'a as the second major group of Islamic sects. It defines them as those who supported Ali and believe he is the Imam after the Prophet by divine designation (النص), which may be explicit (جلي) or implicit (خفي). They hold that the Imamate remains within the line of Ali and his descendants. If the Imamate is claimed by someone else, they consider it either usurpation by oppressors or a case of dissimulation (تقية) from the rightful Imam or his children.

Key Terms
شيعة
إمامة
نص
جلي
خفي
تقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Shi'a are defined by political allegiance to Ali.
Correction: The Shi'a are defined by their belief in a divinely designated Imamate from the Prophet, not mere political support.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the Shi'a belief that Ali is the Imam?
A) Election by the community
B) Designation (النص) by the Prophet
C) Consensus of scholars
D) Conquest

Key Takeaway
The Shi'a are defined by their belief in a divinely designated Imamate, which may be explicit or implicit, and remains within the Prophet's family.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were a Shi'a living under a government that persecuted those who believe in the Imamate of Ali's lineage, would you practice taqiyya? What would that decision reveal about your priorities—survival or truth?
?
The Shi'a believe that true leadership always rightfully belongs to Ali's family, even if others hold power through oppression. Do you hold any belief that remains true even when reality seems to contradict it? If so, how does that affect your daily choices?
?
This passage says the Imamate can depart from Ali's children 'due to a taqiyya from him or his children.' Could you imagine hiding your deepest conviction to protect yourself or your family? At what point does concealment become compromise?

The Ka'biyya: Denial of Divine Will and Vision
Describes the Ka'biyya sect's beliefs about divine will and vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinctive theological positions of the Ka'biyya Mu'tazilite sub-sect, particularly their reinterpretation of divine will and denial of divine vision.

Core Idea
The Ka'biyya denied an attribute of will, reinterpreting 'will' as creation or command.

This passage introduces a specific Mu'tazilite sub-sect, the Ka'biyya, named after Abu al-Qasim al-Ka'bi. Their positions challenge common assumptions about God's will and vision. Understanding their reinterpretations helps clarify the broader theological debates within Mu'tazilism and the diversity of early Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal English translation. Pay attention to how the Ka'biyya define their key terms.
Notice the connection to al-Khayyat, their teacher.
2
Identify the two key claims
Identify the claims about divine will (irada) and divine vision (ru'ya). How do they reinterpret 'will' differently for God's own actions versus the actions of others?
The passage states: 'action occurs without His will' and 'He does not see Himself nor others'.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the Ka'biyya would deny that God wills His own actions. How does their interpretation affect the understanding of God's attributes?
This connects to the broader Mu'tazilite emphasis on divine unity and justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكعبية : هو أبو القاسم بن محمد الكعبي كان من معتزلة بغداد وتلميذ الخياط قالوا : فعل الرب واقع بغير إرادته فإذا قيل : إنه تعالى مريد لأفعاله أريد أنه خالق لها، وإذا قيل : مريد لأفعال غيره أريد أنه آمر بها ولا يرى نفسه ولا غيره إلا أنه يعلمه كما ذهب إليه الخياطية
Al-Ka'biyya: He is Abu al-Qasim bin Muhammad al-Ka'bi, he was from the Mu'tazila of Baghdad and a student of al-Khayyat. They said: The action of the Lord occurs without His will. When it is said that He, exalted, is willing for His actions, it is meant that He is their creator; and when it is said that He is willing for the actions of others, it is meant that He commands them. And He does not see Himself nor others, except that He knows them, as al-Khayyatiyya held.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage defines the Ka'biyya sect, named after Abu al-Qasim al-Ka'bi, a Mu'tazilite from Baghdad and student of al-Khayyat. It outlines their distinctive theological positions: 1. They assert that God's actions occur without His will (irada), meaning He does not will His own acts. 2. When the term 'will' is applied to God's own actions, it is interpreted as 'creating' them, not a separate attribute of will. 3. When applied to the actions of others, 'will' is interpreted as 'commanding' them. 4. They deny that God sees Himself or any other being, although He has knowledge of them. This position follows the earlier Khayyatiyya school.

Key Terms
الكعبية
الخياط
إرادة
فعل الرب
رؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Ka'biyya deny all divine will, but they reinterpret 'will' as creation or command.
Correction: The Ka'biyya do not deny the term 'will' entirely; they reinterpret it to avoid implying a separate attribute in God.

Checkpoint
According to the Ka'biyya, what does it mean when God is said to be 'willing' for His own actions?
A) He has a separate attribute of will.
B) He is the creator of those actions.
C) He commands those actions.
D) He approves of those actions.

Key Takeaway
The Ka'biyya held that God's actions are not willed in the ordinary sense, and He does not see Himself or others.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that the Ka'biyya reduced God's will to merely creating or commanding, do you find any area in your own heart where you subtly limit God's attributes to fit your understanding? What would it cost you to embrace that God's will is truly a limitless, personal attribute beyond your comprehension?
?
The Ka'biyya denied that God sees Himself. Have you ever acted as if God does not see you—perhaps in a moment of private sin or hidden thought? How does affirming that God's vision is real and all-encompassing change the way you live today?
?
Sects like the Ka'biyya arose from intellectual attempts to protect God's transcendence. Are you ever tempted to prioritize your own reasoning over clear revelation when it comes to who God is? What step will you take this week to submit your intellect to the divine attributes as taught by the Salaf?

Juba'iyya on Divine Will and Speech
Describes Juba'iyya on divine will and speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Juba'iyya doctrine that God's will is an accident not in a locus and that God's speech is created in a body.

Core Idea
For the Juba'iyya, God's will and speech are created entities, not attributes subsisting in God.

The Juba'iyya, named after Abu 'Ali al-Jubba'i, held distinctive views on divine attributes. They argued that God's will is an accident (hadith) that does not exist in a locus, and similarly, God's speech is created in a body. This lesson explores these two positions and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key doctrines
Read the passage and find the statements about God's will and God's speech. Notice how they are described as accidents not in a locus.
2
Understand 'accident not in a locus'
Reflect on what it means for something to be hadith la fi mahall. It is a created entity that does not inhere in a subject.
This contrasts with the Sunni view of eternal attributes.
3
Consider implications for divine attributes
Ask yourself: if will and speech are created, what does that imply about God's essence? The Juba'iyya deny that these are eternal attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجبائية : هو أبو علي محمد بن عبد الوهاب الجبائي من معتزلة البصرة قالوا : إرادة الرب حادثة لا في محل والله تعالى مريد بتلك الإرادة موصوف بها والعالم يفنى بفناء لا في محل عند إرادة الله تعالى فناء العالم، والله متكلم بكلام مركب من حروف وأصوات يخلقه الله تعالى في جسم، والمتكلم بذلك الكلام من فعل الكلام وخلقه لا من قام به وحل فيه، ولا يرى الله في الآخرة والعبد خالق لفعله ومرتكب الكبيرة لا مؤمن ولا كافر، وإذا مات بلا توبة يخلد في النار ولا كرامات للأولياء، ويجب على الله لمن يكلف له أي يجب عليه اللطف بالمكلف ورعاية ما هو أصلح له والأنبياء معصومون : وشارك أبو علي فيها أي في الأحكام المذكورة أبا هاشم ثم انفرد عنه بأن الله عالم لذاته بلا صفة هي علم ولا حالة توجب العالمية وكونه تعالى سمعياً بصيراً معناه أنه لا آفة به، ويجوز الإيلام للعوض
Al-Juba'iyya: He is Abu 'Ali Muhammad ibn 'Abd al-Wahhab al-Jubba'i from the Mu'tazila of Basra. They said: The will of the Lord is an accident not in a locus, and God, exalted, is willing by that will and described by it. The world perishes by a perishing not in a locus when God wills the perishing of the world. God speaks with speech composed of letters and sounds; He creates it in a body. The speaker of that speech is the one who performs the speech and creates it, not the one in whom it subsists and indwells. God will not be seen in the afterlife. The servant is the creator of his own action. The committer of a major sin is neither a believer nor a disbeliever. If he dies without repentance, he will abide eternally in Hellfire. There are no miracles (karamat) for saints. It is obligatory upon God for the one whom He charges (al-mukallaf) — meaning, it is obligatory upon Him to perfect his intellect and prepare the causes of obligations, i.e., it is obligatory upon Him to show kindness to the mukallaf and to care for what is most beneficial for him. The prophets are protected (ma'sumun). Abu 'Ali shared with Abu Hashim in these mentioned rulings, then differed from him in that God is knowing by His essence without an attribute that is knowledge or a state that necessitates being knowing; and that His being hearing and seeing means that there is no defect in Him; and that inflicting pain for compensation is permissible.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage describes the Juba'iyya sect, named after Abu 'Ali al-Jubba'i, a Mu'tazilite from Basra. It details their theological positions: 1. God's will is an accident (hadith) not existing in a locus (la fi mahall), and God is described as willing by that will. 2. The world perishes by a perishing that is also not in a locus when God wills its annihilation. 3. God's speech is created, composed of letters and sounds, created in a body; God is the speaker by creating that speech, not by it subsisting in Him. 4. God will not be seen in the afterlife. 5. Humans create their own actions. 6. The committer of a major sin is in an intermediate state (neither believer nor disbeliever). 7. Unrepentant major sinners will be eternally in Hell. 8. Saints do not perform miracles (karamat). 9. God is obligated to do what is best for the responsible person (al-mukallaf). 10. Prophets are infallible. 11. Abu 'Ali agreed with his son Abu Hashim on some points but differed on: God's knowledge is by essence without an attribute; 'hearing' and 'seeing' mean only absence of defect; and inflicting pain for compensation is allowed.

Key Terms
الجبائية
إرادة حادثة
كلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Juba'iyya deny divine will entirely.
Correction: They affirm divine will but as an accident not in a locus, not an eternal attribute.

Checkpoint
How does the Juba'iyya describe God's speech?
A) Eternal attribute
B) Created in a body
C) Uncreated letters
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The Juba'iyya view divine will and speech as created accidents, not eternal attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's will, do you imagine it as something that occurs within Him or as a separate act? How does that affect your trust in His eternal decree?
?
If God's speech were created in a body, as the Juba'iyya claim, how would that change your relationship with the Quran as a timeless revelation? Are you willing to examine your assumptions about the Quran's nature?
?
The Juba'iyya deny attributes subsisting in God's essence. Does this challenge you to reflect on your own understanding of God's transcendence? What steps can you take to refine your belief to avoid anthropomorphism?

Juba'iyya on Human Action, Sin, and Other Doctrines
Describes Juba'iyya on human action, sin, and other doctrines

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Juba'iyya positions on human action, major sins, vision of God, and divine obligations.

Core Idea
Humans create their own actions, and unrepentant major sinners face eternal Hell; God is not seen.

The Juba'iyya held strong views on human free will and divine justice. They believed humans are creators of their actions, that major sinners are in an intermediate state, and that God is obligated to do what is best for His creatures. This lesson covers these distinct doctrines.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and list the doctrines
Read the Arabic and translation, listing each distinct belief mentioned: vision, human action, major sin, saints’ miracles, divine obligations, infallibility, and the unique views.
2
Focus on human action and major sin
Note that the servant is the creator of his action and the major sinner is in an intermediate state.
This is a key Mu'tazilite position.
3
Understand the obligations upon God
The passage states it is obligatory upon God to do what is best (al-lutf) for the mukallaf. Reflect on how this view underlies Mu'tazilite theology.
4
Note the unique views of Abu 'Ali
The end of the passage lists points where Abu 'Ali differed from Abu Hashim: knowledge by essence, hearing/seeing as absence of defect, and permissibility of inflicting pain for compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The prophets are protected (ma'sumun). What happens to a major sinner who dies without repentance according to the Juba'iyya?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The Juba'iyya held that you create your own actions. In areas where you repeatedly fail (anger, lust, procrastination), are you blaming circumstances and feeling powerless, or do you accept responsibility as the 'creator' of those actions? What step will you take this week to exercise your God-given agency to change?

Bahshamiyya on Blame Without Sin and Repentance
Abu Hashim's views on blame without sin and repentance conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Bahshamiyya's distinct views on deserving blame without sin and the conditions for repentance.

Core Idea
Repentance can be invalid if one persists in another sin or lacks ability.

The Bahshamiyya, followers of Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i, held several controversial positions that set them apart from other Mu'tazilites. Among these are their views on blame and repentance. This passage outlines their unique stances and the logical consequences that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on blame without sin
Identify Abu Hashim's claim that one can deserve blame without committing a sin. Note that this is contrary to consensus and wisdom.
2
Understand the repentance conditions
Examine the condition that repentance from a major sin is invalid if one persists in another sin while knowing its ugliness.
This leads to the consequence that informing a disbeliever is invalid if he persists in any sin.
3
Consider the inability condition
Learn that repentance is not valid when the person lacks the ability to commit the sin again. Reflect on the examples of a mute liar and a castrated fornicator.
These examples illustrate the logical application of their principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البهمشمية : انفرد أبو هاشم عن أبيه بإمكان استحقاق الذم والعقاب بلا معصية مع كونه مخالفاً للإجماع والحكمة، وبأنه لا توبة عن كبيرة مع الإصرار على غيرها عالما بقبحه ويلزمه أن لا يصح إعلام الكافر مع أدنى ذنب أصر عليه ولا توبة مع عدم القدرة فلا تصح توبة الكاذب عن كذبه بعدما صار أخرس، ولا توبة الزاني عن زناه بعدما جب ولا يتعلق علم واحد بمعلومين على التفصيل ولله أحوال لا معلومة ولا مجهولة ولا قديمة ولا حادثة قال الآمدي : هذا تناقض إذ لا معنى لكون الشيء حادثاً إلا أنه ليس قديماً ولا معنى لكونه مجهولاً إلا أنه ليس معلوماً على أن إثبات حالة له غير معلومة مما لا سبيل إليه
Al-Bahshamiyya: Abu Hashim differed from his father in [holding] the possibility of deserving blame and punishment without sin, despite it being contrary to consensus and wisdom; and that there is no repentance from a major sin while persisting in another sin while knowing its ugliness, and it follows that informing the disbeliever is not valid with the slightest sin he persists in; and there is no repentance with lack of ability, so the repentance of a liar from his lie after he becomes mute is not valid, nor the repentance of a fornicator from his fornication after he is castrated; and a single knowledge does not relate to two known things in detail; and for God there are states (ahwal) that are neither known nor unknown, neither eternal nor originated. Al-Amidi said: This is a contradiction, for there is no meaning to a thing being originated except that it is not eternal, and no meaning to it being unknown except that it is not known. Moreover, affirming a state that is unknown for Him is something impossible.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage describes the Bahshamiyya sect, named after Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i, and his distinctive views that separate him from his father Abu 'Ali. Abu Hashim held: 1. It is possible to deserve blame and punishment without committing a sin, which the author notes is contrary to consensus and wisdom. 2. Repentance from a major sin is not accepted if one persists in another sin while knowing its ugliness. Consequently, informing a disbeliever is invalid if he persists in even a minor sin. 3. Repentance is not valid when one lacks the ability to commit the sin again (e.g., a liar who becomes mute cannot repent from lying, a fornicator who is castrated cannot repent from fornication). 4. A single act of knowledge cannot encompass two known things in detail. 5. For God, there exist states (ahwal) that are not known, not unknown, not eternal, and not originated. Al-Amidi criticizes this as a contradiction: 'originated' means 'not eternal' and 'unknown' means 'not known', so the definition is circular. Furthermore, affirming an unknown state for God is impossible.

Key Terms
استحقاق الذم
توبة
بلا معصية
البهمشمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Bahshamiyya, what is required for repentance from a major sin to be valid?
A) One must cease all sins immediately.
B) One must not be persisting in another sin while knowing its ugliness.
C) One must have the ability to commit the sin again.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
The Bahshamiyya held that repentance requires both desisting from all known sins and retaining the ability to commit the sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to have repented from a major sin, but you are still indulging in a smaller sin you know is wrong. According to Abu Hashim, is your repentance valid? What does this reveal about the sincerity of your repentance?
?
Think of a sin you committed in the past that you can no longer commit due to changed circumstances (e.g., you are no longer in that environment, or you have lost the ability). Have you truly repented from it, or are you relying on your inability as a substitute for repentance? How does this lesson force you to re-evaluate your repentance?
?
If repentance requires desisting from all sins you know are wrong, which sin are you most unwilling to leave? How does that unwillingness reveal a deeper attachment to sin that your faith must confront? What concrete step will you take today to break that attachment?
